Appointment of a Qasim and Dispute on His Fee Distribution
Ruling on appointing a divider and how his fee is paid, with an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the qasim's fee distribution, explain the reasoning behind each view, and identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling) within the school.

Core Idea
The qasim's fee is divided equally among partners, not proportionally to shares, because his service is indivisible.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the practical matter of appointing a divider (qasim) for joint property. It first recommends that the state appoint a salaried qasim from the public treasury to avoid charging partners. If that is not possible, a private qasim is appointed and his fee must be borne by the partners. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises on how to distribute this fee: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it is divided equally per head, while his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), hold that it should be proportional to each partner's share. The text presents the reasoning of both sides and indicates that Imam's view is the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for qasim appointment
First, distinguish between a state-appointed qasim paid from the treasury (recommended) and a private qasim appointed when that is not possible (fee charged to partners).
The rukn of the act is the appointment itself, not the fee.
2
State the general ruling on appointment from treasury
The text states it is mustahabb (recommended) to appoint a qasim whose sustenance is from the public treasury, so he divides without fee.
This is to avoid suspicion and ease the burden on people.
3
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute on fee distribution
When a private qasim is appointed, his fee must be paid by the partners. Here, Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn diverge: Imam says equal per head; Sahibayn say proportional to shares.
Al-Shafi'i agrees with Sahibayn on this point.
4
Compare the proofs (adillah) of both sides
Sahibayn argue by qiyas: the fee is a maintenance cost, like measuring or weighing, so it should follow share proportion. Imam counters: the fee is for the act of distinguishing shares, which is a single, indivisible service benefiting all partners equally; proportional calculation introduces fractions and difficulty.
Imam's view relies on the nature of the service, not the property.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: for partners with unequal shares (e.g., 1/3 and 2/3), Imam's view gives a 50-50 split, Sahibayn's gives 33-67. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is Imam's view, as it is preferred and simpler.
Tarjih is based on avoiding complexity and following the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text

وندب نصب قاسم رزقه من المال؛ ليقسم بلا أجر، وإلا فينصب قاسم يقسم بأجر بعدد الرؤوس، لا على قدر الأنصباء. فيأخذ حظه إلخ: تفريع على كون القسمة في المثلي إفرازا وتمييزا لحظه، لكن محله إذا كان المثلي مقبوضا للشريكين، أما إذا كان غير مقبوض فلا، كذا في "الحموي" عن "الشلبي". (فتح) وهي في غيره إلخ: أي المبادلة الظاهرة في غير المثلي للتفاوت، فلا يمكن أن يجعل كأنه أخذ عين حقه؛ لعدم المعادلة بينهما بيقين، وإذا كان كذلك، فليس لأحدهما أن يأخذ حظه في غيبة صاحبه. (فتح) وندب نصب قاسم إلخ يعني يستحب نصب قاسم، ورزقه في بيت المال؛ لأن القسمة . القضاء من جنس حيث أنه يتم به قطع المنازعة، فأشبه رزق ،القاضي، ولأن منفعته تعود إلى العامة كمنفعة القضاء والمقاتل والمفتي، فتكون كفايته في بيت المال؛ لأنه أعد لمصالحهم كنفقة هؤلاء، وفي "العتابية" وغيرها: وينصب القاضي قاسما، ويجوز للقاضي أن يقسم بنفسه، ويأخذ على ذلك من المتقاسمين أجرة، وهذا لأن القسمة ليست بقضاء على الحقيقة حتى لا يفترض على القاضي مباشرتها، وإنما الذي يفترض عليه جبر الآبي على القسمة إلا أن لها شبها بالقضاء؛ لأنها تستفاد منه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٩٢/٨]

(عيني) وإلا فينصب قاسم إلخ : يعني إن لم ينصب قاسما رزقه من بيت المال، نصبه وجعل رزقه على المتقاسمين؛ لأن النفع لهم على الخصوص، وليس بقضاء حقيقة حتى جاز للقاضي يأخذ الأجرة على القسمة وإن كان لا يجوز له على القضاء، ألا ترى أنه لا يفترض عليه أن يقسم عليهم بالمباشرة، ومباشرة القضاء فرض عليه، ويقدر له القاضي أجرة مثله كي لا يطمع في أموالهم، ويتحكم بالزيادة، والأفضل أن يكون رزقه من بيت المال؛ لأنه أرفق وأبعد من التهمة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٩٢/٨] بعدد الرؤوس: يعني يجب عليهم الأجرة على عدد الرؤوس ولا يتفاوت بتفاوت الأنصباء، وهذا عند الإمام، وعندهما: على قدر الأنصباء، وهو قول الشافعي، حتى لو كان مال بين اثنين لأحدهما ،ثلثه، وللآخر ثلثاه، فالأجر عليهما نصفان عنده وعندهما يجب أثلاثا لهما أنه مؤونة الملك، فيتقدر بقدره كأجرة الكيال والوزان وحفر البئر المشترك، وله : أن الأجر مقابل بالتمييز، وأنه لا يتفاوت وربما يصعب الحساب بالنظر إلى القليل؛ لكسور فيه، ولا يتصور تمييز قليل من كثير إلا بعد النظر فيهما، فتعلق الحكم بأصل التمييز؛ لأن عمل الإفراز واقع لهما جملة،
=
And it is recommended to appoint a divider whose sustenance is from the treasury, so that he divides without fee; otherwise, a divider is appointed who divides for a fee, distributed equally per head, not according to the shares. ... 'per head': meaning the fee is obligatory upon them according to the number of heads, and it does not vary with variation of shares. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): according to the shares, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i. Even if the property is between two, one having one-third, the other two-thirds, the fee upon them is half-half according to Imam, and according to them it is one-third and two-thirds. According to them, the fee is a maintenance of the property, so it is estimated according to its amount, like the fee of the measurer, weigher, and digging of a shared well. According to Imam: the fee is in exchange for the distinguishing, and it does not vary, and perhaps calculation becomes difficult considering the small shares due to fractions; and distinguishing a small from a large share is not imagined except after looking at both, so the ruling is attached to the principle of distinguishing; because the act of separation occurs for them collectively.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage addresses the practical matter of who bears the cost of the divider (qasim). It is recommended (sunna) that the ruler appoint a qasim whose salary is from the public treasury (Bait al-Mal) so that he divides without charging the partners. If such a paid official is not available, the ruler appoints a qasim who charges a fee, and that fee is divided equally among the partners (per head), not proportionally to their shares. This is the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa. His two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that the fee should be distributed proportionally to each partner's share, like other expenses of the property. The text explains Imam's reasoning: the fee is in exchange for the act of distinguishing shares, which is a single service benefiting all equally, and proportional calculation may be complex due to fractions. The passage also notes the similarity of the qasim to a judge; the qasim's work ends disputes, so like a judge he should ideally be paid from the treasury. However, since qisma is not exactly qada, a judge may take a fee for qisma but not for qada itself. The preferred opinion is that the qasim be from the treasury to avoid suspicion.

Key Terms
القاسم (al-qāsim): The person appointed to divide joint property among partners.
بعدد الرؤوس (bi'adad al-ru'ūs): Equally per person, not proportional to shares.
مؤونة الملك (mu'nat al-milk): Maintenance cost of the property; expenses that preserve or benefit the property.
تمييز (tamyiz): Distinguishing or separating shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, how is the fee of the qasim (divider) distributed among the partners?
A) According to the value of the property
B) According to the number of heads (equally)
C) According to the size of each share
D) According to the difficulty of division

Key Takeaway
The qasim's fee is divided equally among partners according to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, not proportionally to shares.

Partition Based on Claim of Ownership Without Proof
Ruling on partition based on claim vs. possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a claim of ownership is accepted based on apparent possession for partition, and distinguish when proof of ownership is required.

Core Idea
Apparent possession suffices for partition when claiming ownership, but not when only possession is proven.

This passage from the chapter on partition (al-Qismah) addresses a key procedural issue: when co-owners request division, what proof is needed? The Hanafi school holds that if they claim ownership (without specifying how they acquired it), the judge may order partition based on their apparent possession. However, if they only prove that the property is in their hands without claiming ownership, proof of ownership is required because the property might belong to someone else. The text also resolves an intra-madhhab difference between two narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two situations: (1) partners claim ownership and request partition; (2) partners only prove the property is in their hands. Note the different rulings.
The key distinction is whether they assert 'this is our property' (claim of ownership) or simply prove possession.
2
Understand the legal basis
When they claim ownership, the partition is allowed because apparent possession (zaahir al-hal) is taken as evidence, and no third party's right is affected. The sold item already left the seller’s ownership.
The proof 'al-shay' al-zahir anna lahu' is an istihsan basis.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Two narrations exist: al-Quduri (no proof needed when claiming ownership) and al-Jami' al-Saghir (proof required). The author declares the first more correct.
The resolution is that the two narrations actually address different facts—claim vs. mere possession—so there is no real contradiction.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, if partners say 'this is our property', partition proceeds. If they only say 'this is in our hands', they must prove ownership.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of relying on apparent circumstances unless a strong contrary possibility exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل القسمة
المنقول والعقار المشترى ودعوى الملك ولو برهنا أن العقار في أيديهما لم يقسم بأن قالوا: هذا ملكنا. وأجيب عن الأول: الشيء الظاهر أنه له ولأن المبيع زال عن ملك البائع قبل القسمة فلم تكن القسمة قضاء على الغير. وفي رواية: لا يقسم حتى يقيموا البينة على الملك لجواز أن يكون في أيديهم والملك للغير. والأول أصح. وأما في دعوى الملك ولم يذكروا كيفية الانتقال إليهم فلأنه ليس في القسمة قضاء على الغير فإنهم لم يقروا بالملك لغيرهم فيجوز. ثم قيل: هذا قول الإمام، وقيل: قول الكل، وهو الأصح. (مسكين، العيني، تكملة) ولو برهنا أن العقار إلخ أي لو أقام رجلان بينة على أن العقار في أيديهما وطلبا القسمة لم يقسم حتى يقيما البينة على أنه ملك لهما لاحتمال أن يكون لغيرهما. وهذه المسألة بعينها هي المسألة السابقة وهي قوله: "ودعوى الملك" لأن المراد فيها هو أنهم يدعون الملك ولم يذكروا كيف انتقل ولم يشترط فيها إقامة البينة على أنه ملكهم وهو رواية "القدوري" وشرط ههنا وهو رواية "الجامع الصغير". فإن كان قصد المصنف تعيين الروايتين فليس فيه ما يدل على ذلك وإلا فتقع المسألة مكررة يتحاشى عنه مثل هذا المختصر. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٨/٢] هذا الاعتراض بأنه لا تكرار فإن الشركاء في المسألة السابقة وهي رواية "القدوري" ادعوا أنه ملكهم ولا حاجة حينئذ إلى إقامة البينة على أنه ملكهم لأن الأصل أن الأملاك تكون في يد الملاك إذ من في يده شيء يقبل قوله أنه ملكه ما لم ينازعه غيره فيعتبر هذا الظاهر وإن احتمل أن يكون ملك الغير لأنه احتمال بلا دليل. وفي هذه المسألة وهي رواية "الجامع الصغير" ادعوا أنه في أيديهما وظاهر أن كون العقار في أيديهما يحتمل أن يكون بطريق الإجارة أو العارية أو الملك فاحتيج إلى أن يقيموا البينة على أنه ملكهم فافترقت المسألتان ولا شبهة في اختلاف جوابهما.
Issues of Partition: Movable and immovable property purchased, and claiming ownership, and if they prove that the property is in their hands, it is not partitioned unless they say: this is our property. And it was answered concerning the first: the apparent thing is that it is his, and because the sold item left the seller's ownership before partition, so the partition is not a judgment against a third party. In one transmission: it is not partitioned until they establish proof of ownership, because it is possible it is in their hands and the ownership is for another. The first is more correct. As for claiming ownership without mentioning how it was transferred, because there is no judgment against a third party in the partition, as they have not acknowledged ownership for others, so it is permissible. Then it was said: this is the statement of the Imam, and it was said: the statement of all, and this is the most correct. (Miskin, al-Ayni, Takmila) And if they prove that the property etc., meaning if two men establish proof that the property is in their hands and they request partition, it is not partitioned until they establish proof that it is owned by them, due to the possibility that it belongs to others. This very issue is the previous issue, which is his statement: "claiming ownership," because what is meant therein is that they claim ownership and they did not mention how it was transferred, and it was not conditioned therein to establish proof that it is their property, and this is the narration of al-Quduri, and here it is conditioned, and this is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. If the author intended to specify the two narrations, then there is nothing in it to indicate that; otherwise, the issue would be repeated, which a concise work like this avoids. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/348] This objection is that there is no repetition, because the partners in the previous issue, which is the narration of al-Quduri, claimed it is their property, and there is no need then to establish proof that it is their property, because the principle is that properties are in the hands of their owners; whoever has something in his hand, his statement that it is his property is accepted as long as no one disputes with him, so this apparent is considered, even if it is possible it belongs to another, because it is a possibility without evidence. In this issue, which is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, they claimed that it is in their hands, and apparently the property being in their hands could be through lease, loan, or ownership, so they needed to establish proof that it is their property. Thus the two issues differ, and there is no doubt that their answers differ.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion (ruling with proof) from the book of partition (Kitab al-Qismah). The author begins by presenting an issue: If two partners claim ownership of property that is in their hands, and they request partition, the rule depends on how they claim. If they claim ownership without specifying how they acquired it, partition is allowed based on the apparent possession because no third party's rights are affected. However, if they merely prove the property is in their hands without claiming ownership, partition is not granted until they prove ownership, as it could belong to someone else. The text notes two narrations within the Hanafi school: the first (from al-Quduri) does not require proof of ownership when they claim it is theirs; the second (from al-Jami' al-Saghir) requires proof. The author resolves that the first is more correct. A later commentator objects that the two issues are different: in the first, the partners claim ownership (so apparent possession suffices), while in the second, they only prove possession (so proof of ownership is needed). Thus there is no repetition. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that the claim of ownership is accepted based on apparent possession unless there is strong evidence of another's right.

Key Terms
دعوى الملك (da'wa al-milk): A claim of ownership over property without specifying the mode of acquisition.
قسمة (qismah): The division of jointly owned property among the co-owners.
ظاهر الحال (zaahir al-hal): Apparent condition; what is outwardly evident and legally relied upon in the absence of contrary evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when two partners claim ownership of a property in their hands without mentioning how they acquired it, what is the ruling regarding partition?
A) Partition is not allowed until they prove ownership with a valid documentary proof.
B) Partition is allowed based on their claim because the apparent possession supports it, and there is no judgment against a third party.
C) Partition is allowed only if they have witnesses to the purchase.
D) Partition is forbidden because it might affect the rights of an absent heir.

Key Takeaway
A claim of ownership without specification suffices for partition in Hanafi law, but mere possession requires proof of ownership.

Partition with Absent or Minor Heirs: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling on partition when two heirs prove death and number of heirs with an absent or minor co-heir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partition with absent or minor heirs, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding proof, and apply the ruling to a real estate partition scenario.

Core Idea
Partition requires safeguarding absent heirs; Abu Hanifa demands proof, Sahibayn rely on present heirs' statement.

When an inherited property is held by some heirs while others are absent or minors, the judge must ensure the absent party's rights are protected. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the procedure and highlights a key difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Sahibayn) on whether formal proof is required before partition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Two heirs prove the death of the testator and the number of heirs. The property is in their possession, and there is a co-heir who is absent or a minor. The judge partitions the property but appoints an agent (wakil) for the absent or guardian (wasi) for the minor to receive the share.
This is the default Hanafi ruling.
2
Distinguish between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
Imam Abu Hanifa requires the present heirs to establish proof (bayyinah) before the judge because partition is a form of judgment (qada) against the absent/minor. Sahibayn allow the judge to partition based on the statement of the present heirs, with the absent/minor's share set aside.
The mufta bihi (operative) position is Sahibayn's view due to ease.
3
Understand the protective mechanism
The judge must appoint a representative to receive the share of the absent/minor. This ensures the absent/minor's right is not lost and there is someone to manage the property until they claim it.
If no representative is appointed, the partition is defective.
4
Examine extra-judicial partition
If the heirs partition without a judge's decree, it is invalid unless the absent/minor later ratifies it. If the absent/minor dies before ratification, Abu Hanifa accepts ratification by their heirs, while Muhammad al-Shaybani rejects it because the right to ratify dies with the person.
This shows the strictness of Muhammad al-Shaybani's position.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
In contemporary practice, the judge may proceed with partition upon the request of the present heirs without requiring them to formally prove death and heirship, provided an agent is appointed for the absent/minor.
This follows the Sahibayn's view for practicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو برهنا إلخ : يعني لو برهن رجلان على موت المورث وعدد الورثة والحال أن الدار في أيديهما ومعهما وارث غائب أو صبي قسم القاضي العقار بينهما ولكن نصب وكيلا للغائب أو وصيا للصبي يقبض نصيبه؛ لأن في نصبه نظرا للصغير والغائب. ولابد من إقامة البينة عند الإمام لما بينا؛ لأن في هذه القسمة قضاء على الغائب والصغير. وعندهما يقسم بقولهما لما ذكرنا، ويعزل حق الغائب والصغير ويشهد أنه قسمها بينهم بإقرار الكبار الحاضرين فإن الغائب والصغير على حجتهما. وأفاد بقوله: "قسم" أن القاضي فعل ذلك. قال في "المحيط": فلو قسما بغير قضاء لم تجز القسمة إلا أن يحضرا فيجيزا، أو يبلغ فيجيزا. فإن مات الغائب أو الصغير فأجاز ورثته جاز عند الإمام، وقال محمد: لا يجوز؛ لأنه مات من له الإجازة فبطلت. وللإمام أنا لو أبطلنا القسمة بالموت احتجنا إلى إعادة مثلها فإجازتها أولى. [تكملة البحر الرائق: 8/294] (عيني، فتح) والدار في أيديهما ومعهما وارث غائب أو صبي قسم بطلبهما ونصب وكيلا أو وصيا بقبض نصيبه. كذا في بعض نسخ "الكنز" وهو المتقرر في نسخ المطبوعة. ووقع في أكثر النسخ مكان "أيديهما ومعهما" "أيديهم ومعهم" بصيغة الجمع. قال في "الفتح": قيل: إنه سهو والصواب "في أيديهما ومعهما" كما في بعض النسخ. وأجيب بأن المراد بالجمع التثنية على حد قوله تعالى: {فقد صغت قلوبكما} [التحريم: 4]، وتعقب بأن فيه لبسا بخلاف الآية وجواز هذه الإرادة عند الأمن من اللبس. وأقول القرينة على إرادة التثنية ما سيأتي من أنه لا يقسم إذا كان في يد الوارث الغائب، فدعوى اللبس ممنوعة. (فتح باختصار)
And if they prove etc.: meaning if two men prove the death of the testator and the number of heirs, and the house is in their hands, and with them is an absent heir or a minor, the judge partitions the property between them but appoints an agent for the absent one or a guardian for the minor to take his share, because in his appointment there is consideration for the minor and the absent. It is necessary to establish proof according to Imam Abu Hanifa for what we have explained, because in this partition there is a judgment against the absent and the minor. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he partitions based on their statement, as we mentioned, and he sets aside the share of the absent and the minor, and he bears witness that he partitioned it among them by the admission of the adult present, because the absent and the minor are entitled to their proof. And his statement 'he partitioned' indicates that the judge did that. It is said in al-Muhit: If they partition without a judicial decree, the partition is not valid unless they (the absent or minor) attend and approve it, or the minor reaches puberty and approves it. If the absent or minor dies and his heirs approve, it is valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad said: it is not valid, because the one who had the approval died, so it is void. And for Imam (Abu Hanifa): if we invalidate the partition due to death, we would need to repeat it, so approving it is better. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/294) (Ayni, Fath) And the house is in their hands and with them is an absent heir or a minor, he partitions at their request and appoints an agent or guardian to take his share. Thus in some copies of al-Kanz, which is the established version in printed copies. In most copies, instead of 'their hands (dual) and with them (dual)' it is 'their hands (plural) and with them (plural)'. It was said in al-Fath: it is said it is a mistake, and the correct is 'in their hands (dual) and with them (dual)' as in some copies. And it was answered that the plural is intended as dual, like His statement: {فقد صغت قلوبكما} [al-Tahrim:4], and it was objected that there is ambiguity unlike the verse, and its permissibility is when there is no fear of ambiguity. I say the qarina for intending the dual is what follows, that it is not partitioned if it is in the hand of the absent heir, so the claim of ambiguity is rejected. (Fath in summary)
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion (ruling with proof) regarding partition when there are only two heirs who prove the death and the number of heirs, but there is an absent or minor co-heir. The judge has the authority to partition, but he must appoint an agent (wakil) for the absent heir or a guardian (wasi) for the minor to take his share. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the establishment of proof (bayyinah) is required before the judge, because this partition constitutes a judgment (qada) against the absent and minor. However, according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the judge may partition based on the statement of the present heirs, as the absent and minor's share is set aside and they retain their right to proof later. The text also discusses a scenario where the partners partition without a judge: it is invalid until the absent or minor ratifies it. If they die before ratification, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the heirs' ratification suffices, while Muhammad holds it is invalid. The commentary also clarifies that the dual form ('aydihima' instead of 'aydihim') is intended because only two heirs are present. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that partition involving absent or minor heirs requires safeguards for their rights.

Key Terms
الجيم الصغير (al-jāmiʿ al-ṣaghīr): A collection of rulings by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, forming part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.
وكيل (wakīl): A person appointed to act on behalf of another in legal matters.
قضاء (qaḍā): Judgment or decree issued by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what must the judge do when two heirs request partition of an inheritance and there is an absent heir?
A) The judge refuses partition until the absent heir appears.
B) The judge partitions and gives the absent heir's share to the present heirs.
C) The judge partitions but appoints an agent to receive the absent heir's share.
D) The judge partitions only if the absent heir's whereabouts are unknown.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, partition with absent/minor heirs is valid provided the judge safeguards their rights through appointment of a representative, with the Sahibayn's view (no formal proof required) being the operative position.

Three Cases Where Partition is Not Allowed
Lists three scenarios where partition is not allowed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scenarios where partition of jointly owned property is invalid according to the Hanafi school and explain why representation (khasm) is required.

Core Idea
Partition of joint property is invalid when any partner is absent without representation.

This lesson examines the conditions for valid partition in Hanafi law, focusing on three specific prohibitions derived from the principle that a judgment cannot be passed against an absentee without a legal representative. The text distinguishes between inheritance and purchase to explain why representation differs. Understanding these cases is essential for applying rules of joint property and protecting the rights of absent owners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is partition (qismah) of jointly owned property. The ruling is that it is not allowed (la yuqsam) in three specific situations. The hukm level is batil (invalid).
The ruling applies even if proof of ownership is established.
2
Recall the three scenarios
List the three cases from the text: (1) purchasers with one absent, (2) property in the hand of an absent heir or child, (3) only one heir present.
All three share the same common issue.
3
Understand the proof: distinction between inheritance and purchase
In inheritance, a present heir can represent the deceased, so partition is valid even if some heirs are absent. In purchase, each owner has direct independent ownership, so no one can represent an absent co-owner.
This is a talil (legal reasoning) based on the nature of ownership.
4
Apply the requirement of representation (khasm)
Partition involves a judicial decision. If an absent party has no representative, the court cannot adjudicate their share. Hence, each case fails due to lack of representation.
Even a minor or depositary does not serve as a proper representative.
5
Note the practical implication
The rule protects absent owners from having their property divided without their input. It applies regardless of whether proof is established.
This is the correct view (fi al-sahih).

As for ownership established by purchase, each one has a direct cause in his share, therefore he does not return due to defect against the seller of his seller, so the present one does not become a representative for the absent one; then the proof (bayyinah) regarding the absent one is established without a representative, so it is not accepted. As for when the property is in the hand of the absent heir, because the partition is a judgment against the absent by removing the thing from his hand without a representative for him, so it is not permissible. Similarly if part of it is in his hand and the rest in the hand of the present one, and similarly if it is in the hand of his depositary or borrower or in the hand of the minor, because the depositary and minor are not representatives. And there is no difference in this between establishing proof and not, in the correct view. As for when one heir is present, because there is no representative with him, and he is only a representative for himself.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion (ruling with proof) presenting three cases where partition is not allowed: (1) when the partners are purchasers and one is absent, (2) when the property is in the hand of an absent heir or a minor, and (3) when only one heir is present. The common reason is that partition would constitute a judgment against an absent party without a proper representative. In the case of purchasers, the distinction from inheritance is that each purchaser's ownership is independent and based on his own cause of acquisition, so the present purchaser does not represent the absent one, unlike heirs where ownership is derivative from the deceased, allowing one heir to represent the deceased. In the case of property in the hand of an absent heir or minor, partition would remove property from their possession without a representative, which is unjust. Similarly, when only one heir is present, there is no other present to act as a representative for the absent ones. The text emphasizes that this applies even if proof is established, because the issue is the absence of a proper litigant.

Key Terms
قضـاء على الغائب (qaḍāʾ ʿalā al-ghāʾib): A judicial decision that affects an absent person without their representation.
خصم (khaṣm): A litigant or representative in a legal dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the absent heir is not present to choose an evaluator.

Key Takeaway
Partition of joint property is invalid when any co-owner is absent without a legal representative, due to the prohibition of judgment against an absentee.

Partition and Harm: When the Judge Compels Division
States rulings on partition when it harms all, some, or none of the co-owners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of partition based on benefit/harm and identify which co-owners can compel division according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Partition is not compelled if it destroys benefit for all; only the owner of a large share can compel when some benefit and some are harmed.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rules on compulsory partition (qismah jabriyyah) when co-owners disagree. The central criterion is benefit (manfa'ah): if every co-owner will benefit from the division, the judge compels it. If all are harmed, he does not. When some benefit and some are harmed, the request of the owner with a large share is granted, but not that of the small share owner. This ruling is rooted in preventing obstinacy (ta'annut) and wasting property, as elaborated by later Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Partition (qismah) of jointly owned property is the act under discussion. The ruling varies depending on whether all, some, or none of the co-owners benefit from the division.
The matn states three scenarios.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is not a specific verse or hadith but the principle that division completes benefit (takmil al-manfa'ah). If it destroys benefit, it is futile and causes harm, which the judge should not facilitate.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the objectives of property rights.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The ruling links benefit to compulsion: when all benefit, division is their right and the judge compels; when all are harmed, the request is from an obstructing party (mut'ant) and is rejected; when some benefit and some are harmed, the large share owner's benefit is prioritized over the small share owner's harm.
The reasoning uses the concept of hazz kathir (large share).
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Al-Khassaf held that the judge divides only at the request of the large share owner. Al-Jassas said the opposite, and al-Hakim said either can compel. Al-Khassaf's view is considered more correct (asahh) by later authorities.
This ikhtilaf is captured in the madhhab dispute map.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is that of al-Khassaf, endorsed by al-Hidayah and Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The judge thus refuses the request of the small share owner as futile and obstructive.
This is the position adopted for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم بطلب أحدهم لو انتفع كل بنصيبه، وإن تضرر الكل لم يقسم إلا برضائهم، وإن انتفع البعض وتضرر البعض لقلة حظه ، قسم بطلب ذي الكثير فقط .
المنفعة
لأنه
وقسم إلخ لأن فيه تكميل المنفعة إذا كان كل واحد منهم ينتفع بنصيبه بعد القسمة، وكانت القسمة حقا لهم، فوجب على القاضي إجابتهم، قال في "العناية": يعني يقسم جبرا، ومراده: إذا كان من جنس واحد؛ لأن فيه معنى الإفراز؛ لتفاوت المقاصد تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٩٧/٨]
وإن تضرر الكل إلخ: أي إن تضرر الكل بالقسمة كما لو طلبوا قسمة البئر والرحى والحمام والحائط لم يقسم إلا برضاء الجميع؛ لأن القسمة لتكميل المنفعة، وفي قسمة هذا تفويتها، فيعود على موضوعها بالنقض، ولأن الطالب للقسمة متعنت ويريد إدخال الضرر على غيره، فلا يجيبه الحاكم إلى ذلك؛ لأنه اشتغال بما لا يفيد بل بما يضر، ويجوز بالتراضي؛ لأن الحق لهم، وهم أعرف بحاجتهم لكن القاضي لا يباشر ذلك، وإن طلبوه منه؛ لأن القاضي لا يشتغل بما لا فائدة فيه لاسيما إذا كان فيه إضرار وإضاعة مال؛ لأن ذلك حرام، ولا يمنعهم منه، وظاهر المتن أن القاضي يقسم عند رضاهم، وفي الينابيع" و"الذخيرة" : ذكر شيخ الإسلام: أن القاضي لا يقسم، وبعض المشايخ قال يقسم فظهر أن في المسألة روايتين تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۹۷/۸] قسم بطلب إلخ يعني يقسم بطلب صاحب الحظ الكثير القسمة فقط، لا بطلب صاحب الحظ القليل كذا ذكره الخصاف له، وذكر الجصاص له عكس هذا، وذكر الحاكم في "مختصره: أن أيهما طلب القسمة يقسم القاضي، وما ذكره الخصاف له أصح وجه قول الخصاف أن صاحب الكثير يطلب من القاضي أن يخصه بالانتفاع بملكه، فوجب على القاضي أن يجيبه إلى ذلك، ولا يعتبر ضرر الآخر؛ يريد أن ينتفع . بملك غيره، فلا يمكن من ذلك، ولو طلب صاحب القليل مع أ أنه لا ينتفع بحقه . مع أنه متعنت في طلب القسمة، فلا يشتغل القاضي بما لا يفيد، ولم يتعرض المؤلف لما إذا كان كل واحد منهم لا ينتفع بالقسمة؛ لقلة حظه، قال في "المبسوط": بيت بين رجلين أراد أحدهما القسمة وامتنع الآخر، والبيت صغير لا ينتفع به واحد منهما، لا يجيبهما القاضي إلى ذلك، والأصح أنه لا يقسم إلا إذا طلب صاحب الكثير خاصة، ومنهم من صحح ما ذكره الحاكم، والأول أصح كذا في "التكملة" بتغيير واختصار. فإن قلت: مسألة المتن منافية؛ لما ذكروا أن شرط القسمة عدم فوت المنفعة كما بيناها في أول الكتاب وهنا فات مع أنه يقسم بطلب ذي الكثير؟ قلت ليس المراد بما ذكروا فوت المنفعة بالنسبة لأحد النصيبين، بل بالنسبة لهما معا دل على ذلك ما في "الشرنبلالية" بحيث قال مصرحا على الشرط المذكور: فلهذا لا يقسم حائط وحمام ونحوهما بطلب أحدهما، فافهم.
المنفعة
لأنه
And division at the request of one of them if each benefits from his share. And if all are harmed, it is not divided except by their consent. And if some benefit and some are harmed due to the smallness of his share, it is divided only at the request of the one with the large share. Benefit because. 'And division etc.' because it involves completion of benefit when each of them benefits from his share after division, and division is their right, so the judge must grant their request. Al-Inayah said: He means he divides forcibly, and his intent is when it is of one type because it involves separation due to differing objectives. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/397). 'And if all are harmed etc.': i.e., if all are harmed by division, as if they request division of a well, a mill, a bathhouse, or a wall, it is not divided except by the consent of all. Because division is to complete benefit, and dividing this removes benefit, so it defeats its purpose. And because the one requesting division is obstinate and wants to harm others, so the judge does not grant his request because it is engaging in what is not beneficial but harmful. However, it is permissible by mutual consent because the right is theirs, and they know their needs better. But the judge does not undertake it even if they ask him, because the judge does not engage in what has no benefit, especially if it involves harm and waste of wealth, as that is prohibited. But he does not prevent them from doing it themselves. The apparent meaning of the text is that the judge divides when they consent. In al-Yanabi' and al-Dhakhira: Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that the judge does not divide, and some shaykhs said he does, so there are two narrations on the issue. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/297). 'Division at the request etc.': means it is divided only at the request of the owner of the large share, not at the request of the owner of the small share. Thus mentioned by al-Khassaf. Al-Jassas mentioned the opposite. Al-Hakim in his Mukhtasar said that whichever of them requests division, the judge divides. What al-Khassaf mentioned is more correct. The reasoning of al-Khassaf is that the owner of the large share asks the judge to allocate him the benefit of his property, so the judge must grant that, and the harm to the other is not considered; the other wants to benefit from someone else's property, so he is not allowed that. If the owner of the small share requests, although he does not benefit from his right, he is obstinate in requesting division, so the judge does not engage in what is not beneficial. The author did not address the case where each does not benefit from division due to the smallness of his share. Al-Mabsut said: A house between two men; one wants division and the other refuses, and the house is small so neither benefits from it, the judge does not grant them that. The correct view is that it is not divided unless the owner of the large share specifically requests. Some corrected what al-Hakim mentioned, but the first is more correct. So in al-Takmila, with modification and abridgement. If you say: The issue of the text is contradictory to what they mentioned that the condition of division is that benefit is not lost, as we explained at the beginning of the book, and here benefit is lost yet it is divided at the request of the one with the large share? I say: What they meant by loss of benefit is not relative to one of the shares, but to both of them together. That is indicated by what is in al-Sharnablaliyya, where he explicitly stated on the mentioned condition: For this reason, a wall, a bathhouse, and the like are not divided at the request of one of them. Understand. Benefit. Because.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on when partition (qismah) is mandatory, voluntary, or prohibited based on benefit/harm. The matn states three scenarios: (1) If every co-owner benefits from partition, the judge divides at anyone's request. (2) If all are harmed (e.g., dividing a well, mill, bathhouse, wall), it is not divided except with everyone's consent. (3) If some benefit and some are harmed (due to small share), it is divided only at the request of the one with the large share. The commentary expands on each: In the first case, division completes benefit, so the judge compels it if property is of one type. In the second, division destroys benefit, so the one requesting is obstinate; the judge refuses but may allow if all consent (with an ikhtilif on whether judge performs it). In the third, the large share owner has a right to access his property, while the small share owner seeks to harm; thus only the large share owner's request is granted. The commentary resolves an apparent contradiction: the condition for division is that benefit is not lost relative to both shares, not just one. Hence, a wall or bathhouse is not divided on request of one because benefit is lost for both.

Key Terms
منفعة (manfa'ah): Benefit or utility derived from property; the ability to use one's share productively.
متعنت (mut'ant): One who is obstinate or seeks to cause difficulty, here by demanding a partition that harms others.
حظ كثير (hazz kathir): A large share; the co-owner whose portion is substantial enough to benefit from partition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the judge refuse to divide a small house when neither co-owner would benefit from partition?
A) Because the house is too valuable
B) Because partition would not benefit either, so the request is futile
C) Because one co-owner is absent
D) Because they must both agree

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of compulsory partition hinges on whether each co-owner benefits; the judge must not facilitate harm or obstinacy.

When Can a Judge Compel Partition? The Hanafi Benefit-Harm Rule
Explains when a judge can compel partition based on benefit or harm to co-owners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios for compulsory partition and apply the Hanafi condition of benefit preservation to determine whether a judge must compel.

Core Idea
Compulsory partition requires benefit for all; if some are harmed, only the large-share owner can compel.

A judge cannot simply order a partition whenever a co-owner requests it. The law must balance the rights of ownership against the harm that division can cause. This passage explains the precise conditions under which a judge may compel a partition, focusing on whether each co-owner will benefit from the division. It also addresses a practical problem: what if only one owner has a substantial share?

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The text distinguishes three cases: (1) each co-owner benefits from partition, (2) all are harmed, (3) some benefit but one is harmed due to a very small share. Note the ruling for each.
2
Understand the rationale for each ruling
For case 1, partition completes benefit, which is the purpose of partition. For case 2, partition would destroy benefit, so it is not compelled. For case 3, the large-share owner stands to benefit substantially, while the small-share owner's harm is not considered because he would not benefit anyway and is seen as obstinate.
The text emphasizes that the judge does not engage in futile or harmful actions.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on consent-based division
The passage notes that some Hanafi authorities (Shaykh al-Islam) hold that the judge should not divide even when all consent, while others allow it. Note the two riwayat and the apparent contradiction in the text.
The relied-upon view (zahir al-matan) allows division with consent, but the judge may decline.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction regarding preservation of benefit
The author reconciles a potential conflict: the condition of preservation of benefit applies to both shares together, not individually. Therefore, a large-share owner's request is granted even if the other loses benefit, because the large owner's benefit is real and the small owner's loss is negligible or self-inflicted.
This nuance is critical for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل القسمة
وقسم بطلب أحدهم لو انتفع كل بنصيبه، وإن تضرر الكل لم يقسم إلا برضائهم، وإن انتفع البعض وتضرر البعض لقلة حظه، قسم بطلب ذي الكثير فقط .
فليس أحد خصما عن الميت وعن الغائب، وإن كان خصما عنهما، فليس أحد يخاصمه عن نفسه ليقيم البينة عليه، بخلاف ما لو كان الحاضر من الورثة اثنين حيث تكون القسمة قضاء بحضرة المتخاصمين. (فتح، تكملة) وقسم إلخ لأن فيه تكميل المنفعة إذا كان كل واحد منهم ينتفع بنصيبه بعد القسمة، وكانت القسمة حقا لهم، فوجب على القاضي إجابتهم، قال في "العناية": يعني يقسم جبرا، ومراده: إذا كان من جنس واحد؛ لأن فيه معنى الإفراز؛ لتفاوت المقاصد تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٩٧/٨]
وإن تضرر الكل إلخ: أي إن تضرر الكل بالقسمة كما لو طلبوا قسمة البئر والرحى والحمام والحائط لم يقسم إلا برضاء الجميع؛ لأن القسمة لتكميل المنفعة، وفي قسمة هذا تفويتها، فيعود على موضوعها بالنقض، ولأن الطالب للقسمة متعنت ويريد إدخال الضرر على غيره، فلا يجيبه الحاكم إلى ذلك؛ لأنه اشتغال بما لا يفيد بل بما يضر، ويجوز بالتراضي؛ لأن الحق لهم، وهم أعرف بحاجتهم لكن القاضي لا يباشر ذلك، وإن طلبوه منه؛ لأن القاضي لا يشتغل بما لا فائدة فيه لاسيما إذا كان فيه إضرار وإضاعة مال؛ لأن ذلك حرام، ولا يمنعهم منه، وظاهر المتن أن القاضي يقسم عند رضاهم، وفي الينابيع" و"الذخيرة" : ذكر شيخ الإسلام: أن القاضي لا يقسم، وبعض المشايخ قال يقسم فظهر أن في المسألة روايتين تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۹۷/۸] قسم بطلب إلخ يعني يقسم بطلب صاحب الحظ الكثير القسمة فقط، لا بطلب صاحب الحظ القليل كذا ذكره الخصاف له، وذكر الجصاص له عكس هذا، وذكر الحاكم في "مختصره: أن أيهما طلب القسمة يقسم القاضي، وما ذكره الخصاف له أصح وجه قول الخصاف أن صاحب الكثير يطلب من القاضي أن يخصه بالانتفاع بملكه، فوجب على القاضي أن يجيبه إلى ذلك، ولا يعتبر ضرر الآخر؛ يريد أن ينتفع بملك غيره، فلا يمكن من ذلك، ولو طلب صاحب القليل مع أنه لا ينتفع بحقه مع أنه متعنت في طلب القسمة، فلا يشتغل القاضي بما لا يفيد، ولم يتعرض المؤلف لما إذا كان كل واحد منهم لا ينتفع بالقسمة؛ لقلة حظه، قال في "المبسوط": بيت بين رجلين أراد أحدهما القسمة وامتنع الآخر، والبيت صغير لا ينتفع به واحد منهما، لا يجيبهما القاضي إلى ذلك، والأصح أنه لا يقسم إلا إذا طلب صاحب الكثير خاصة، ومنهم من صحح ما ذكره الحاكم، والأول أصح كذا في "التكملة" بتغيير واختصار. فإن قلت: مسألة المتن منافية؛ لما ذكروا أن شرط القسمة عدم فوت المنفعة كما بيناها في أول الكتاب وهنا فات مع أنه يقسم بطلب ذي الكثير؟ قلت ليس المراد بما ذكروا فوت المنفعة بالنسبة لأحد النصيبين، بل بالنسبة لهما معا دل على ذلك ما في "الشرنبلالية" بحيث قال مصرحا على الشرط المذكور: فلهذا لا يقسم حائط وحمام ونحوهما بطلب أحدهما، فافهم.
Chapter of Partition. Matters of partition. And if partition is requested by one of them, if each benefits from his share, [the judge compels]; and if all are harmed, it is not divided except with their consent. And if some benefit and some are harmed due to the smallness of his share, it is divided only at the request of the one with the large share. So no one is a representative for the deceased or the absent, and if they were representatives, no one could argue against himself to establish evidence, unlike when there are two heirs present where the partition is adjudicated in the presence of the litigants. (Fath, Takmila) 'And partition, etc.' because it completes the benefit when each benefits from his share after division, and partition is their right, so the judge must respond to them. He said in al-'Inaya: he means it is divided by compulsion, and his intent is when it is of one type, because it contains the meaning of separation due to differing objectives. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 8/397) 'And if all are harmed, etc.': i.e., if all are harmed by the partition, as if they request division of a well, mill, bathhouse, or wall, it is not divided except with the consent of all; because partition is to complete the benefit, and in dividing these the benefit is lost, so it reverts to its subject matter with nullification, and because the requester is obstinate and wants to cause harm to others, so the judge does not respond to that, as it is occupation with what is not beneficial rather it is harmful. But it is permissible by mutual consent because the right is theirs, and they know their need better, but the judge does not undertake that even if they ask him, because the judge does not occupy himself with what has no benefit, especially if it involves harm and waste of wealth, as that is forbidden. The apparent meaning of the text is that the judge divides when they consent, and in al-Yanabi' and al-Dhakhira, Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that the judge does not divide, and some shaykhs said he does divide, so it appears there are two narrations on the matter. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 8/297) 'Division at the request of, etc.': means he divides only at the request of the owner of the large share, not at the request of the owner of the small share; thus al-Khassaf narrated it, and al-Jassas narrated the opposite, and al-Hakim in his Mukhtasar said that whichever of them requests division the judge divides. What al-Khassaf narrated is more correct. The argument of al-Khassaf is that the owner of the large share asks the judge to allocate to him the benefit of his property, so the judge must respond to that, and the harm to the other is not considered, as he wants to benefit from the property of another and cannot be allowed. And if the owner of the small share requests, while he does not benefit from his right and is obstinate, the judge does not occupy himself with what is not beneficial. The author did not address the case where none of them benefits from division due to the smallness of their shares. He said in al-Mabsut: a house between two men, one wants division and the other refuses, and the house is small, neither benefits from it. The judge does not respond to them. The most correct is that he does not divide except if the owner of the large share specifically requests. Some corrected what al-Hakim mentioned, but the first is more correct, as in al-Takmila with modification and abbreviation. If you say: the case in the text contradicts what they mentioned that the condition of partition is the preservation of benefit, as we explained at the beginning of the book, and here it is lost yet he divides at the request of the one with the large share? I say: what they meant is not loss of benefit relative to one of the two shares, but relative to both together, as indicated by what is in al-Sharnablaliyya where he clearly stated regarding the mentioned condition: for this reason a wall, bathhouse, and the like are not divided at the request of one of them. Understand thus.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which a judge can compel partition of jointly owned property. The ruling distinguishes three scenarios: 1) If each co-owner benefits from the division, the judge compels it. 2) If all are harmed, it requires consent of all. 3) If some benefit and some are harmed due to a small share, only the owner of the large share can compel division. The reasoning is based on the purpose of partition: it exists to complete benefit. If all are harmed, compelling partition would cause harm without benefit, which is not permitted. If only a small-share owner requests, he would not benefit and is considered obstinate, so his request is not entertained. The passage also discusses conflicting narrations and opinions among Hanafi scholars (al-Khassaf, al-Jassas, al-Hakim) on whether the judge can divide when all are harmed but consent, and whether the small-share owner's request ever triggers compulsion. The relied-upon position (al-ashahh) is that the judge does not compel without the large-share owner's request unless all consent. The author clarifies a potential contradiction: the principle that partition requires preservation of benefit applies to both shares together, not individually. Hence, a large-share owner's request is granted even if the other loses benefit, provided the large-share owner's benefit is substantial.

Key Terms
قسمة (qisma): Division of jointly owned property among co-owners.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion by the judge; the power to force a division against a co-owner's will.
تراضي (tarāḍī): Mutual consent of all co-owners to the partition.
منفعة (manfaʿa): Benefit; the utility or profit derived from property.
متعنت (mutaʿannit): One who is obstinate or seeks to cause harm without legitimate benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if two co-owners share a wall, and one requests partition, but the partition would harm both (neither can use their share), what is the ruling?
A) The judge compels partition even if both are harmed.
B) The judge does not compel partition unless both consent.
C) The judge compels partition at the request of the one with the larger share.
D) The judge always compels partition when requested.

Key Takeaway
Compulsory partition in Hanafi law is conditioned on benefit: the judge compels only when the division actually serves the purpose of partition without causing overall harm.

Compulsory Partition: When Co-Owners Can Be Forced to Divide
Ruling with proof on compulsory partition conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for compulsory partition of goods and apply it to distinguish between items divisible by compulsion and those requiring consent.

Core Idea
Only goods of one type can be divided by compulsion; mixed types, jewels, slaves, doves, wells, mills require consent.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out a key principle of Hanafi partnership law: not all jointly owned property can be forced into division. The decisive factor is whether the division preserves the same benefit for each party. When the goods are of one type, compulsion is allowed; otherwise, mutual consent is needed. We will explore the rule, its rationale, and its exceptions, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its condition
State the general rule: goods (ʿurūḍ) of one type (jins wāḥid) are divisible by compulsion; two or more types require consent. The condition is unity of type.
2
Understand the rationale (talīl)
Why does unity of type allow compulsion? Because the benefit (manfaʿa) is similar for both types, so division is mere separation (tamyīz). If types differ, division becomes a exchange (muʿāwaḍa) causing loss of benefit to the unwilling party, hence invalid unless by consent.
This is the core principle: compelled division must preserve the same benefit.
3
Apply to specific items
List the items that are excluded from compulsion: jewels (gross disparity and ignorance), slaves (hidden qualities – per Abu Hanifa), dovecote, well, mill (harm to all). For each, understand why the condition of equal benefit fails.
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on slaves; Abu Hanifa's view is the muftā bihi.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on slaves
Abu Hanifa: slaves are not divisible by compulsion due to hidden qualities (maʿānī bāṭina). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: slaves are one type, like animals, so compulsion allowed. The three other Imams concur with the Sahibayn. The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view when slaves stand alone.
When slaves are mixed with other goods or of mixed gender, consensus may apply.
5
Note exceptional cases
Even for dovecote and well, if both co-owners consent and request division from the judge, there are two narrations on whether the judge may divide. The passage alludes to permissibility due to some benefit.
This nuance shows that consent relaxes conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقسم العروض من جنس واحد ولا يقسم الجنسان والجواهر والرقيق والحمام والبئر والرحى إلا برضائهم .
ويقسم العروض إلخ : أي يقسم العروض إذا كانت من جنس واحد جبرا؛ لأن اعتبار المبادلة في المنفعة المالية ممكن عند اتحاد الجنس؛ لاتحاد المقصود فيه، فيقع تمييزا، فيملك القاضي الإجبار عليها تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٩٨/٨] ولا يقسم إلخ: أي لا يقسم الجنسان والجواهر وما عطف عليهما من الرقيق والحمام والبئر والرحى إلا برضاء الشركاء، فقوله: "إلا "برضائهم" متعلق بالجميع يعني لا يملك القاضي الإجبار على قسمة الجنسين بأن يقسم الأجناس المختلفة قسمة بأن يجمع نصيب أحدهم في الإبل والآخر في البقر ؛ لما في ذلك من تفويت جنس المنفعة على الآبي؛ لأن قبل القسمة كان له المنفعة في الجنسين جميعا، وبعد القسمة فات منفعة أحدهما، فلم تقع القسمة تمييزا بل معاوضة، وهي لا تجوز إلا بالتراضي.
فإن قيل: شرط صحة القسمة أن ينتفع بالمقسوم كما كان قبل القسمة؟ قلت: ذاك شرط الجبر عليها لا شرط القسمة بالتراضي، وكذا لا يقسم الجواهر جبرا؛ لفحش التفاوت، ولأن جهالة الجواهر أفحش من جهالة الرقيق، ولهذا لو تزوج على لؤلؤة أو ياقوتة، أو خالع عليها لا تصح التسمية، ولو تزوج أو خالع على عبد يصح، فأولى أن لا يجبر على القسمة، وقيل: لا يقسم الكبار منها؛ لفحش التفاوت ويقسم الصغار؛ لقلة التفاوت، وقيل: إن اختلف جنسهما لا يقسم، وإن اتحد يقسم كسائر الأجناس. (فتح، تكملة بتوضيح واختصار) والرقيق: أي لا يقسم الرقيق أيضا من غير تراض عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يجوز؛ لاتحاد الجنس، فصار كالإبل والخيل والغنم، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله: أن قسمة الرقيق متعذر لمعانيها الباطنة ولا وقوف عليها، ولا يمكن التعديل، فلا يقسم إلا بتراض، وهذا الخلاف فيما إذا كان الرقيق وحدهم وليس معهم شيء آخر من العروض، وهم ذكور فقط، أو إناث فقط، وأما إذا كانوا مختلطين بين الذكور والإناث لا يقسم بالإجماع، وإن كان مع الرقيق شيء آخر مما يقسم، جازت القسمة في الرقيق تبعا لغيرهم بالإجماع. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٩/٢] والحمام إلخ أي لا تقسم هذه الثلاثة إلا برضائهم لما ذكر من إلحاق الضرر بالكل، ولو اقتسما الحمام أو البئر بأنفسهم جاز، ولكل واحد نوع منفعة بأن يتخذ نصيبه من الحمام بيتا وإن طلبا جميعا القسمة من القاضي هل يقسم؟ فيه روايتان في رواية لا يقسم؛ لأنها تضمنت تفويت منفعة، وليس للقاضي ذلك؛ لأنه يكون سفها يمكنه، وفي رواية: يقسم؛ لأنهم رضوا بذلك، وإليه أشار في الكتاب؛ لأنه فيه نوع منفعة كذا في "المحيط". تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۹۹/۸] إلا برضائهم : متعلق بالمسائل الست المذكورة من قوله: "ولا يقسم".
And goods of one type are divided, but two types, jewels, slaves, doves, wells, and mills are not divided except with their consent. And goods etc.: i.e., goods are divided by compulsion when they are of one type, because consideration of exchange in financial benefit is possible when the type is unified due to unified intent, so it falls as separation, so the judge has authority to compel it. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 8/298) And not divided etc.: i.e., two types and jewels and what is coupled with them of slaves, doves, wells, and mills are not divided except with the consent of the partners. So his saying 'except with their consent' relates to all, meaning the judge does not have authority to compel division of two types by dividing different types such that one's share is in camels and the other's in cattle, because that causes loss of the type of benefit for the unwilling party; because before division he had benefit in both types, and after division the benefit of one type is lost, so the division is not separation but exchange, which is not permissible except by mutual consent. If it is said: the condition for validity of division is that one benefits from the divided property as before division? I say: that is a condition for compulsion, not for division by consent. Similarly, jewels are not divided by compulsion due to gross disparity, and because the ignorance regarding jewels is more gross than ignorance regarding slaves, and for this reason if one marries for a pearl or a ruby, or divorces for them, the specification is not valid, while if he marries or divorces for a slave, it is valid, so a fortiori he cannot be compelled to divide. It is also said: the large ones are not divided due to gross disparity, and the small ones are divided due to slight disparity. And it is said: if their types differ, they are not divided; if they are unified, they are divided like other types. (Fath, Takmila with clarification and abbreviation) And slaves: i.e., slaves are also not divided without mutual consent according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two disciples it is permissible due to unity of type, so they become like camels, horses, and sheep, and the three (Imams) concur with them. His argument is that division of slaves is difficult due to their inner qualities which are not known and cannot be equalized, so it is not divided except by consent. This difference is when the slaves are alone without any other goods, and they are all males or all females. If they are mixed between males and females, it is not divided by consensus. If there is something else with the slaves that can be divided, division of slaves is permissible as a subsidiary to others by consensus. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq, 2/349) And doves etc.: i.e., these three are not divided except with their consent due to the mentioned harm to all. If they divide the dovecote or well among themselves, it is permissible, and each has a type of benefit, such as making his share of the dovecote into a house. If they both request division from the judge, does he divide? There are two narrations: in one narration he does not divide because it entails loss of benefit, and the judge does not have that right because it would be foolishness if he could; in another narration he divides because they consented to that, and the author alluded to this in the book because there is some benefit in it. Thus in al-Muhit. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, 8/299) 'Except with their consent' relates to the six issues mentioned from his saying 'and not divided'.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage details which joint property can be partitioned by compulsion and which requires mutual consent. The key principle is that compelled partition is only valid if it preserves the benefit for each co-owner. Goods (ʿurūḍ) of one type can be compelled because their purposes are similar, making exchange of benefit possible. But if types differ (e.g., camels vs. cattle), forced division would deprive one party of a type of benefit, effectively becoming an exchange (muʿāwaḍa) rather than mere separation (tamyīz), which requires consent. Jewels are not divisible by compulsion due to extreme disparity and uncertainty (jahāla). Slaves: Abu Hanifa holds they cannot be compelled because their internal qualities are hidden and equalization is impossible; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow compulsion as slaves are of one type like animals. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view when slaves are alone, but if mixed with other goods, they are divided as a subsidiary. Doves, wells, and mills cause harm to all if divided, so they require consent. The passage also notes differing narrations on whether the judge can divide a dove-cote or well if both parties consent and request it.

Key Terms
عروض (ʿurūḍ): Goods, chattel, or movable property other than real estate; often includes livestock, merchandise, etc.
جنس واحد (jins wāḥid): One type or category of goods; items with similar usage and value.
جنسان (jinsān): Two different types; different categories of goods.
جواهر (jawāhir): Precious stones (jewels) like pearls and rubies.
رقيق (raqīq): Slaves; enslaved human beings.
حمام (ḥamām): Dovecote or pigeon house; also doves themselves.
بئر (biʾr): Well; water source.
رحى (raḥā): Mill; grinding stone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following items is NOT divisible by compulsion, even if of one type?
A) A herd of camels
B) A flock of sheep
C) A collection of pearls
D) A set of horses

Key Takeaway
Compulsory partition is only allowed when the property is of one type (jins wāḥid) and the benefit is preserved; mixed types, jewels, slaves (per Abu Hanifa), dovecotes, wells, and mills require mutual consent.

Compulsory vs Consensual Division: Same Type and Different Type Goods
Opening of the chapter stating the rule for goods of one type vs two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between compulsory and consensual division and apply the condition of same type to determine if the judge may compel division.

Core Idea
When goods are of the same type, division is pure demarcation; when types differ, it becomes exchange requiring consent.

The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between divisions that merely separate shares and those that effectively exchange one type of property for another. The key is the unity of type (jins). This lesson unpacks why a judge can compel division of one type but not of different types, and why jewels are treated with extra caution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
The act is dividing jointly owned goods. Determine whether it is compulsory (jabran) or consensual (bi rida).
Compulsory division is ordered by the judge even if a partner refuses.
2
Check the condition: unity of type (ittihad al-jins)
If the goods are all of the same type (e.g., all cows), then exchange of benefit is possible without loss; the division is mere demarcation (tamyiz).
The text says: لاتحاد المقصود فيه، فيقع تمييزا
3
Contrast: different types (al-jinsan)
If the goods are of different types (e.g., camels and cows), dividing them compels a partner to lose one type of benefit – this is exchange (mu'awadah) and requires mutual consent.
The judge cannot impose such a division.
4
Apply the rule to jewels
Jewels are not divisible compulsorily because of excessive disparity and hidden qualities. Even small jewels may be divided if disparity is low, but the default is no compulsion.
Large jewels: no compulsion; small ones: may be divided if similar.
5
Identify the dalil
The proof is based on the possibility of exchange in financial benefit when type is united. This is rational reasoning (qiyas) and istihsan for benefit.
The dalil block shows the reasoning: اعتبار المبادلة في المنفعة المالية ممكن عند اتحاد الجنس
